<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Carbon monoxide</b> (<b>CO</b>) is a colorless, odorless, and tasteless gas that is slightly less dense than air. It is toxic to <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobic</a> animals (both <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin#Analogues_in_non-vertebrate_organisms" title="Hemoglobin">invertebrate</a> and vertebrate, including humans) when encountered in concentrations above about 35 <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">ppm</a>, although it is also produced in normal animal metabolism in low quantities, and is thought to have some normal biological functions. In the atmosphere, it is spatially variable and short lived, having a role in the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground-level_ozone" title="Ground-level ozone">ground-level ozone</a>.
</p><p>Carbon monoxide consists of one <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> atom and one <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atom, connected by a <a href="/wiki/Triple_bond" title="Triple bond">triple bond</a> that consists of two <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a> as well as one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipolar_bond" title="Dipolar bond">dative</a> covalent bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It is the simplest <a href="/wiki/Oxocarbon" title="Oxocarbon">oxocarbon</a> and is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> with other triply-bonded diatomic molecules having ten valence electrons, including the <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> anion, the <a href="/wiki/Nitrosonium" title="Nitrosonium">nitrosonium</a> cation and molecular <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> the carbon monoxide <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">carbonyl</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384–322 BC) first recorded that burning coals produced toxic fumes. An ancient method of execution was to shut the criminal in a bathing room with smoldering coals. What was not known was the mechanism of death. Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> (129–199 AD) speculated that there was a change in the composition of the air that caused harm when inhaled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In 1776, the French chemist <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph-Marie-Fran%C3%A7ois_de_Lassone" title="fr:Joseph-Marie-François de Lassone">de Lassone</a> produced CO by heating <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a> with <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>, but mistakenly concluded that the gaseous product was <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, as it burned with a blue flame. The gas was identified as a compound containing <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> by the Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Cruickshank_(chemist)" title="William Cruickshank (chemist)">William Cruikshank</a> in 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Its toxic properties on dogs were thoroughly investigated by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Bernard" title="Claude Bernard">Claude Bernard</a> around 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, a gas mixture including carbon monoxide was used to keep <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">motor vehicles</a> running in parts of the world where <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a> were scarce. External (with a few exceptions) charcoal or <a href="/wiki/Wood_gas_generator" title="Wood gas generator">wood gas generators</a> were fitted, and the mixture of atmospheric nitrogen, hydrogen, carbon monoxide, and small amounts of other gases produced by <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">gasification</a> was piped to a gas mixer. The gas mixture produced by this process is known as <a href="/wiki/Wood_gas" title="Wood gas">wood gas</a>. Carbon monoxide was also used on a large scale during <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> at some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extermination_camps_in_the_Holocaust" title="Extermination camps in the Holocaust">Nazi German extermination camps</a>, the most notable by <a href="/wiki/Gas_van" title="Gas van">gas vans</a> in <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno_extermination_camp" title="Chełmno extermination camp">Chełmno</a>, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Action_T4" title="Action T4">Action T4</a> "<a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>" program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carbon monoxide is produced from the partial oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>-containing compounds; it forms when there is not enough oxygen to produce <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>), such as when operating a <a href="/wiki/Stove" title="Stove">stove</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> in an enclosed space. In the presence of oxygen, including atmospheric concentrations, carbon monoxide burns with a blue flame, producing carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">Coal gas</a>, which was widely used before the 1960s for domestic lighting, cooking, and heating, had carbon monoxide as a significant fuel constituent. Some processes in modern technology, such as <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">iron smelting</a>, still produce carbon monoxide as a byproduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> A large quantity of CO byproduct is formed during the oxidative processes for the production of chemicals. For this reason, the process off-gases have to be purified. On the other hand, considerable research efforts are made in order to optimize the process conditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> develop catalyst with improved selectivity <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and to understand the reaction pathways leading to the target product and side products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Worldwide, the largest source of carbon monoxide is natural in origin, due to <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemical</a> reactions in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> that generate about 5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">12</span></sup> kilograms per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Other natural sources of CO include volcanoes, forest fires, other forms of combustion, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide-releasing_molecules" title="Carbon monoxide-releasing molecules">carbon monoxide-releasing molecules</a>.
</p><p>In biology, carbon monoxide is naturally produced by the action of <a href="/wiki/Heme_oxygenase" title="Heme oxygenase">heme oxygenase</a> 1 and 2 on the <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> breakdown. This process produces a certain amount of <a href="/wiki/Carboxyhemoglobin" title="Carboxyhemoglobin">carboxyhemoglobin</a> in normal persons, even if they do not breathe any carbon monoxide. Following the first report that carbon monoxide is a normal <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> in 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verma_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-verma-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-19">[19]</a></sup> as well as one of three gases that naturally modulate <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammatory responses</a> in the body (the other two being <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>), carbon monoxide has received a great deal of clinical attention as a biological regulator. In many tissues, all three gases are known to act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-inflammatories" title="Anti-inflammatories">anti-inflammatories</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasodilators" title="Vasodilators">vasodilators</a>, and promoters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neovascular" title="Neovascular">neovascular</a> growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2009_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2009-20">[20]</a></sup> Clinical trials of small amounts of carbon monoxide as a drug are ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clinical-trial_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-clinical-trial-21">[21]</a></sup> Too much carbon monoxide causes <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide poisoning</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_properties">Molecular properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Molecular properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carbon monoxide has a <a href="/wiki/Molar_mass" title="Molar mass">molar mass</a> of 28.0, which, according to the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a>, makes it slightly less dense than air, whose average molar mass is 28.8.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bond_length" title="Bond length">bond length</a> between the carbon atom and the oxygen atom is 112.8 <a href="/wiki/Picometre" title="Picometre">pm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gilliam_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-gilliam-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This bond length is consistent with a triple bond, as in molecular <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (N<sub>2</sub>), which has a similar bond length (109.76 pm) and nearly the same <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular mass</a>. Carbon–oxygen double bonds are significantly longer, 120.8 pm in <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The boiling point (82 K) and melting point (68 K) are very similar to those of N<sub>2</sub> (77 K and 63 K, respectively). The <a href="/wiki/Bond-dissociation_energy" title="Bond-dissociation energy">bond-dissociation energy</a> of 1072 kJ/mol is stronger than that of N<sub>2</sub> (942 kJ/mol) and represents the strongest chemical bond known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_state" title="Electronic state">electronic state</a> of carbon monoxide is a <a href="/wiki/Singlet_state" title="Singlet state">singlet state</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> since there are no unpaired electrons.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonding_and_dipole_moment">Bonding and dipole moment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Bonding and dipole moment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The carbon monoxide has a very high <a href="/wiki/Bond-dissociation_energy" title="Bond-dissociation energy">bond-dissociation energy</a>, the strongest of any neutral molecule, 11.65 eV.  Carbon and oxygen together have a total of 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_shell" title="Valence shell">valence shell</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Octet_rule" title="Octet rule">octet rule</a> for both carbon and oxygen, the two atoms form a <a href="/wiki/Triple_bond" title="Triple bond">triple bond</a>, with six shared electrons in three bonding molecular orbitals, rather than the usual double bond found in organic carbonyl compounds. Since four of the shared electrons come from the oxygen atom and only two from carbon, one bonding orbital is occupied by two electrons from oxygen, forming a dative or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipolar_bond" title="Dipolar bond">dipolar bond</a>. This causes a C←O <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polarization</a> of the molecule, with a small negative charge on carbon and a small positive charge on oxygen. The other two bonding orbitals are each occupied by one electron from carbon and one from oxygen, forming (polar) covalent bonds with a reverse C→O polarization, since oxygen is more <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegative</a> than carbon. In the free carbon monoxide, a net negative charge δ<sup>–</sup> remains at the carbon end and the molecule has a small <a href="/wiki/Bond_dipole_moment" title="Bond dipole moment">dipole moment</a> of 0.122 <a href="/wiki/Debye" title="Debye">D</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The molecule is therefore asymmetric: oxygen has more electron density than carbon, and is also slightly positively charged compared to carbon being negative. By contrast, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> dinitrogen molecule has no dipole moment.
</p><p>If carbon monoxide acts as a <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">ligand</a>, the polarity of the dipole may reverse with a net negative charge on the oxygen end, depending on the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
See also the section <i><a href="#Coordination_chemistry">"Coordination chemistry"</a></i> below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bond_polarity_and_oxidation_state">Bond polarity and oxidation state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bond polarity and oxidation state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Theoretical and experimental studies show that, despite the greater electronegativity of oxygen, the dipole moment points from the more-negative carbon end to the more-positive oxygen end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The three bonds are in fact <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_covalent_bond" title="Polar covalent bond">polar covalent bonds</a> that are strongly polarized. The calculated polarization toward the oxygen atom is 71% for the <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">σ-bond</a> and 77% for both <a href="/wiki/Pi_bond" title="Pi bond">π-bonds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stefan_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stefan-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of carbon in carbon monoxide is +2 in each of these structures. It is calculated by counting all the bonding electrons as belonging to the more electronegative oxygen. Only the two non-bonding electrons on carbon are assigned to carbon. In this count, carbon then has only two valence electrons in the molecule compared to four in the free atom.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_and_physiological_properties">Biological and physiological properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biological and physiological properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">Carbon monoxide poisoning</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">Carbon monoxide poisoning</a> is the most common type of fatal air poisoning in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toxicology2002-omaye_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toxicology2002-omaye-32">[32]</a></sup> Carbon monoxide is colorless, odorless, and tasteless, but highly toxic. It combines with <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Carboxyhemoglobin" title="Carboxyhemoglobin">carboxyhemoglobin</a>, which usurps the space in hemoglobin that normally carries oxygen, but is ineffective for delivering oxygen to bodily tissues. Concentrations as low as 667 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_notation" title="Parts per notation">ppm</a> may cause up to 50% of the body's hemoglobin to convert to carboxyhemoglobin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> A level of 50% carboxyhemoglobin may result in seizure, coma, and fatality. In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">OSHA</a> limits long-term workplace exposure levels above 50 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common symptoms of carbon monoxide poisoning may resemble other types of poisonings and infections, including symptoms such as <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dizziness" title="Dizziness">dizziness</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a>, and a feeling of weakness. Affected families often believe they are victims of food poisoning. Infants may be irritable and feed poorly. Neurological signs include confusion, disorientation, visual disturbance, <a href="/wiki/Syncope_(medicine)" title="Syncope (medicine)">syncope</a> (fainting), and seizures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blumenthal_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blumenthal-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some descriptions of carbon monoxide poisoning include <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retinal</a> hemorrhages, and an abnormal cherry-red blood hue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In most clinical diagnoses these signs are seldom noticed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blumenthal_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blumenthal-35">[35]</a></sup> One difficulty with the usefulness of this cherry-red effect is that it corrects, or masks, what would otherwise be an unhealthy appearance, since the chief effect of removing deoxygenated hemoglobin is to make an asphyxiated person appear more normal, or a dead person appear more lifelike, similar to the effect of red colorants in embalming fluid. The "false" or unphysiologic red-coloring effect in anoxic CO-poisoned tissue is related to the meat-coloring commercial use of carbon monoxide, discussed below.
</p><p>Carbon monoxide also binds to other molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrial</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytochrome_oxidase" title="Cytochrome oxidase">cytochrome oxidase</a>. Exposures to carbon monoxide may cause significant damage to the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>, especially to the <a href="/wiki/Globus_pallidus" title="Globus pallidus">globus pallidus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> often with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sequelae" title="Sequelae">long-term chronic pathological conditions</a>. Carbon monoxide may have severe adverse effects on the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> of a pregnant woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Normal_human_physiology">Normal human physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Normal human physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_signaling_molecules" title="Gaseous signaling molecules">Gaseous signaling molecules</a></div>
<p>Carbon monoxide is produced naturally by the human body as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signaling_molecule" title="Signaling molecule">signaling molecule</a>. Thus, carbon monoxide may have a physiological role in the body, such as a <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">blood vessel relaxant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endogenous_co_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-endogenous_co-39">[39]</a></sup> Because of carbon monoxide's role in the body, abnormalities in its metabolism have been linked to a variety of diseases, including neurodegenerations, hypertension, heart failure, and pathological inflammation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endogenous_co_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-endogenous_co-39">[39]</a></sup> Relative to inflammation, carbon monoxide has been shown to inhibit the movement of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukocyte" title="Leukocyte">leukocytes</a> to inflamed tissues, stimulate leukocyte <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a> of bacteria, and reduce the production of pro-inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> by leukocytes. In animal model studies, furthermore, carbon monoxide reduced the severity of experimentally induced bacterial <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, pancreatitis, hepatic ischemia/reperfusion injury, colitis, osteoarthritis, lung injury, lung transplantation rejection, and neuropathic pain while promoting skin wound healing. These actions are similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Specialized_pro-resolving_mediators" title="Specialized pro-resolving mediators">Specialized pro-resolving mediators</a> which act to dampen, reverse, and repair the tissue damage due to diverse inflammation responses. Indeed, carbon monoxide can act additively with one of these mediators (<a href="/wiki/Resolvin" title="Resolvin">Resolvin</a> D1) to limit inflammatory responses. The studies implicate carbon monoxide as a physiological contributor to limiting inflammation and suggest that its delivery by inhalation or carbon monoxide-forming drugs may be therapeutically useful for controlling pathological inflammatory responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26095908_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26095908-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26208702_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26208702-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26498251_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26498251-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27052783_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27052783-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>CO functions as an endogenous signaling molecule, modulates functions of the cardiovascular system, inhibits blood platelet aggregation and adhesion, suppresses, reverses, and repairs the damage caused by inflammatory responses.
It may play a role as potential therapeutic agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26095908_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26095908-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microbiology">Microbiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Microbiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is a nutrient for <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> archaea, which reduce it to methane using hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> This is the theme for the emerging field of <a href="/wiki/Bioorganometallic_chemistry" title="Bioorganometallic chemistry">bioorganometallic chemistry</a>. Extremophile micro-organisms can, thus, utilize carbon monoxide in such locations as the thermal vents of volcanoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some microbes can convert carbon monoxide to carbon dioxide to yield energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>In bacteria, carbon monoxide is produced via the <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> of carbon dioxide by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_dehydrogenase" title="Carbon monoxide dehydrogenase">carbon monoxide dehydrogenase</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Fe</a>-<a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">Ni</a>-<a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a>-containing protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>CooA is a carbon monoxide sensor protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The scope of its biological role is still unknown; it may be part of a signaling pathway in bacteria and archaea. Its occurrence in mammals is not established.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:472px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/MOP_CO_M.ogv/472px--MOP_CO_M.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:472px;height:316px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="41.25" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="MOP_CO_M.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/MOP_CO_M.ogv" type="video/ogg; <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_presence">Atmospheric presence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Atmospheric presence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:300px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Carbon_Monoxide.ogv/854px--Carbon_Monoxide.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:428px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="48.533333333333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Carbon_Monoxide.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/0e/Carbon_Monoxide.ogv/Carbon_Monoxide.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm;
