<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1801" data-file-width="2402" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg/220px-Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg/330px-Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg/440px-Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Monoxide_concentrations_in_spring..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon Monoxide concentrations in Northern Hemisphere spring as measured with the MOPITT instrument.</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon monoxide (CO) is present in small amounts (about 80 ppb) in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a>. About half of the carbon monoxide in Earth's atmosphere is from the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> and biomass (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fires" title="Forest fires">forest</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bushfire" title="Bushfire">bushfires</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seinfeld2006_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seinfeld2006-58">[58]</a></sup> Most of the rest of carbon monoxide comes from chemical reactions with <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> emitted by human activities and plants. Small amounts are also emitted from the ocean, and from geological activity because carbon monoxide occurs dissolved in molten volcanic rock at high <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressures</a> in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Because natural sources of carbon monoxide are so variable from year to year, it is difficult to accurately measure natural emissions of the gas.
</p><p>Carbon monoxide has an indirect effect on <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> by elevating concentrations of direct <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">tropospheric</a> <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>. CO can react chemically with other atmospheric constituents (primarily the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a>, OH<sup><b>.</b></sup>) that would otherwise destroy methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Through natural processes in the atmosphere, it is eventually oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and ozone. Carbon monoxide is both short-lived in the atmosphere (with an average lifetime of about one to two months) and spatially variable in concentration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">atmosphere of Venus</a> carbon monoxide occurs as a result of the photodissociation of carbon dioxide by electromagnetic radiation of wavelengths shorter than 169 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>.
</p><p>Due to its long lifetime in the mid-troposphere, carbon monoxide is also used as tracer of transport for pollutant plumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_pollution">Urban pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Urban pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is a temporary atmospheric pollutant in some urban areas, chiefly from the exhaust of internal combustion engines (including vehicles, portable and back-up generators, lawn mowers, power washers, etc.), but also from incomplete combustion of various other fuels (including wood, coal, charcoal, oil, paraffin, propane, natural gas, and trash).
</p><p>Large CO pollution events can be observed from space over cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_ground-level_ozone_formation">Role in ground-level ozone formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Role in ground-level ozone formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone" title="Tropospheric ozone">Tropospheric ozone</a></div>
<p>Carbon monoxide is, along with <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>, part of the series of cycles of chemical reactions that form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_smog" title="Photochemical smog">photochemical smog</a>. It reacts with hydroxyl radical (<sup>•</sup>OH) to produce a radical intermediate <sup>•</sup>HOCO, which transfers rapidly its radical hydrogen to O<sub>2</sub> to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peroxy" title="Peroxy">peroxy</a> radical (HO<sub>2</sub><sup>•</sup>) and carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Peroxy radical subsequently reacts with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a> (NO) to form <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> (NO<sub>2</sub>) and hydroxyl radical. NO<sub>2</sub> gives O(<sup>3</sup>P) via photolysis, thereby forming O<sub>3</sub> following reaction with O<sub>2</sub>.
Since hydroxyl radical is formed during the formation of NO<sub>2</sub>, the balance of the sequence of chemical reactions starting with carbon monoxide and leading to the formation of ozone is:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + 2O<sub>2</sub> + hν → CO<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>(where hν refers to the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> of light absorbed by the NO<sub>2</sub> molecule in the sequence)
</p><p>Although the creation of NO<sub>2</sub> is the critical step leading to low level <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> formation, it also increases this ozone in another, somewhat mutually exclusive way, by reducing the quantity of NO that is available to react with ozone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NA-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indoor_pollution">Indoor pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Indoor pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In closed environments, the concentration of carbon monoxide can easily rise to lethal levels. On average, 170 people in the United States die every year from carbon monoxide produced by non-automotive consumer products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpsc_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-cpsc-66">[66]</a></sup> However, according to the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Health" title="Florida Department of Health">Florida Department of Health</a>, "every year more than 500 Americans die from accidental exposure to carbon monoxide and thousands more across the U.S. require emergency medical care for non-fatal carbon monoxide poisoning"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPHT_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPHT-67">[67]</a></sup> These products include malfunctioning fuel-burning appliances such as furnaces, ranges, water heaters, and <a href="/wiki/Gas_heater" title="Gas heater">gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerosene_heater" title="Kerosene heater">kerosene</a> room heaters; engine-powered equipment such as portable generators; fireplaces; and charcoal that is burned in homes and other enclosed areas. The American Association of Poison Control Centers (AAPCC) reported 15,769 cases of carbon monoxide poisoning resulting in 39 deaths in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In 2005, the CPSC reported 94 generator-related carbon monoxide poisoning deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpsc_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-cpsc-66">[66]</a></sup> Forty-seven of these deaths were known to have occurred during power outages due to severe weather, including <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpsc_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-cpsc-66">[66]</a></sup> Still others die from carbon monoxide produced by non-consumer products, such as cars left running in attached garages. The <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> estimates that several thousand people go to hospital emergency rooms every year to be treated for carbon monoxide poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presence_in_blood">Presence in blood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Presence in blood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is absorbed through breathing and enters the blood stream through gas exchange in the lungs. It is also produced in <a href="/wiki/Heme#Degradation" title="Heme">heme catabolism</a> and enters the blood from the tissues, and thus is present in all normal tissues, even if not inhaled.
</p><p>Normal circulating levels in the blood are 0% to 3%,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="what is this the percentage of? Clearly not of blood volume or mass! (August 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> are higher in smokers. Carbon monoxide levels cannot be assessed through a physical exam. Laboratory testing requires a blood sample (arterial or venous) and laboratory analysis on a CO-Oximeter. Additionally, a noninvasive carboxyhemoglobin (SpCO) test method from Pulse CO-Oximetry exists and has been validated compared to invasive methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space">Space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Outside of Earth, carbon monoxide is the second-most common molecule in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a>.  Because of its asymmetry, this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar molecule</a> produces far brighter <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> than the hydrogen molecule, making CO much easier to detect.  Interstellar CO was first detected with <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescopes</a> in 1970. It is now the most commonly used tracer of molecular gas in general in the interstellar medium of galaxies, as molecular hydrogen can only be detected using ultraviolet light, which requires <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_telescopes" title="Space telescopes">space telescopes</a>. Carbon monoxide observations provide much of the information about the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> in which most <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">stars form</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beta_Pictoris" title="Beta Pictoris">Beta Pictoris</a>, the second brightest <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Pictor" title="Pictor">Pictor</a>, shows an <a href="/wiki/Infrared_excess" title="Infrared excess">excess of infrared emission</a> compared to normal stars of its type, which is caused by large quantities of dust and gas (including carbon monoxide)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT-20140308_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAT-20140308-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140306_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140306-74">[74]</a></sup> near the star.
</p><p>Solid carbon monoxide is a component of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a> is about 15% carbon monoxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yeomans_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yeomans-76">[76]</a></sup> It has also been identified spectroscopy on the surface of Neptune's moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lellouchde_Bergh2010_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lellouchde_Bergh2010-77">[77]</a></sup> At room temperature and at atmospheric pressure carbon monoxide is actually only metastable (see <a href="/wiki/Boudouard_reaction" title="Boudouard reaction">Boudouard reaction</a>) and the same is true at low temperatures where CO and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> are solid, but nevertheless it can exist for billions of years in comets. However, there is very little CO in the atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, which seems to have been formed from comets. This may be because there is (or was) liquid water inside Pluto. Carbon monoxide can react with water to form carbon dioxide and hydrogen:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>This is called the <a href="/wiki/Water-gas_shift_reaction" title="Water-gas shift reaction">water-gas shift reaction</a> when occurring in the gas phase, but it can also take place (very slowly) in aqueous solution.
If the hydrogen partial pressure is high enough (for instance in an underground sea), <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a> will be formed:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → HCOOH</dd></dl>
<p>These reactions can take place in only a few million years even at temperatures such as found at Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Miners refer to carbon monoxide as "white damp" or the "silent killer". It can be found in confined areas of poor ventilation in both surface mines and underground mines. The most common sources of carbon monoxide in mining operations are the internal combustion engine and explosives however in coal mines carbon monoxide can also be found due to the low temperature oxidation of coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many methods have been developed for carbon monoxide's production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_production">Industrial production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Industrial production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A major industrial source of CO is <a href="/wiki/Producer_gas" title="Producer gas">producer gas</a>, a mixture containing mostly carbon monoxide and nitrogen, formed by combustion of carbon in air at high temperature when there is an excess of carbon. In an oven, air is passed through a bed of <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>. The initially produced CO<sub>2</sub> equilibrates with the remaining hot carbon to give CO. The reaction of CO<sub>2</sub> with carbon to give CO is described as the <a href="/wiki/Boudouard_reaction" title="Boudouard reaction">Boudouard reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Above 800 °C, CO is the predominant product:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub> + C → 2 CO (ΔH = 170 kJ/mol)</dd></dl>
<p>Another source is "<a href="/wiki/Water_gas" title="Water gas">water gas</a>", a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and carbon monoxide produced via the endothermic reaction of <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> and carbon:
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>O + C → H<sub>2</sub> + CO (ΔH = +131 kJ/mol)</dd></dl>
<p>Other similar "<a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">synthesis gases</a>" can be obtained from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and other fuels.
</p><p>Carbon monoxide can also be produced by <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_electrolysis" title="High-temperature electrolysis">high-temperature electrolysis</a> of carbon dioxide with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_oxide_electrolyzer_cell" title="Solid oxide electrolyzer cell">solid oxide electrolyzer cells</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CO<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO + O<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Carbon monoxide is also a byproduct of the reduction of metal <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a> with carbon, shown in a simplified form as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd>MO + C → M + CO</dd></dl>
<p>Carbon monoxide is also produced by the direct oxidation of carbon in a limited supply of oxygen or air.
</p>
<dl><dd>2 C(s) + O<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO(g)</dd></dl>
<p>Since CO is a gas, the reduction process can be driven by heating, exploiting the positive (favorable) <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> of reaction. The <a href="/wiki/Ellingham_diagram" title="Ellingham diagram">Ellingham diagram</a> shows that CO formation is favored over CO<sub>2</sub> in high temperatures.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_preparation">Laboratory preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Laboratory preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is conveniently produced in the laboratory by the <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oxalic_acid" title="Oxalic acid">oxalic acid</a>, for example with concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koch_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-koch-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coleman_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-coleman-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georg_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Georg-85">[85]</a></sup> Another method is heating an intimate mixture of powdered <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> metal and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>, which releases CO and leaves behind <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>Zn + CaCO<sub>3</sub> → ZnO + CaO + CO</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">Silver nitrate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodoform" title="Iodoform">iodoform</a> also afford carbon monoxide:
</p>
<dl><dd>CHI<sub>3</sub> + 3AgNO<sub>3</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → 3HNO<sub>3</sub> + CO + 3AgI</dd></dl>
<p>Finally, metal <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a> salts release CO upon heating, leaving a <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> as byproduct:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CO</span></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_chemistry">Coordination chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Coordination chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">Metal carbonyl</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MO_COeng.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="341" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/MO_COeng.svg/200px-MO_COeng.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/MO_COeng.svg/300px-MO_COeng.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/MO_COeng.svg/400px-MO_COeng.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MO_COeng.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Energy level scheme of the σ and π orbitals of carbon monoxide</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="1100" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png/150px-Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png/225px-Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png/300px-Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-monoxide-HOMO-phase-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/HOMO/LUMO" title="HOMO/LUMO">HOMO</a> of CO is a σ <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital" title="Molecular orbital">MO</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png/150px-Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png/225px-Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png/300px-Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-monoxide-LUMO-phase-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/HOMO/LUMO" title="HOMO/LUMO">LUMO</a> of CO is a π* <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibonding" title="Antibonding">antibonding</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital" title="Molecular orbital">MO</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Most metals form <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> containing covalently attached carbon monoxide. Only metals in lower oxidation states will complex with carbon monoxide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligands" title="Ligands">ligands</a>. This is because there must be sufficient electron density to facilitate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Back-donation" title="Back-donation">back-donation</a> from the metal d<sub>xz</sub>-orbital, to the π* <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital" title="Molecular orbital">molecular orbital</a> from CO. The lone pair on the carbon atom in CO, also donates electron density to the d<sub>x²−y²</sub> on the metal to form a <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">sigma bond</a>. This electron donation is also exhibited with the <a href="/wiki/Cis_effect" title="Cis effect">cis effect</a>, or the labilization of CO ligands in the cis position. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel_carbonyl" title="Nickel carbonyl">Nickel carbonyl</a>, for example, forms by the direct combination of carbon monoxide and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> metal:
</p>
<dl><dd>Ni + 4 CO → Ni(CO)<sub>4</sub> (1 bar, 55 °C)</dd></dl>
<p>For this reason, nickel in any tubing or part must not come into prolonged contact with carbon monoxide. Nickel carbonyl decomposes readily back to Ni and CO upon contact with hot surfaces, and this method is used for the industrial purification of <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mond_process" title="Mond process">Mond process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In nickel carbonyl and other carbonyls, the electron pair on the carbon interacts with the metal; the carbon monoxide donates the electron pair to the metal. In these situations, carbon monoxide is called the <b>carbonyl</b> <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>. One of the most important metal carbonyls is <a href="/wiki/Iron_pentacarbonyl" title="Iron pentacarbonyl">iron pentacarbonyl</a>, Fe(CO)<sub>5</sub>:
</p><p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IronPentacarbonylStructure.png" title="Structure of iron pentacarbonyl."><img alt="Structure of iron pentacarbonyl." data-file-height="730" data-file-width="903" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/IronPentacarbonylStructure.png/150px-IronPentacarbonylStructure.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/IronPentacarbonylStructure.png/225px-IronPentacarbonylStructure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/IronPentacarbonylStructure.png/300px-IronPentacarbonylStructure.png 2x" width="150"/></a>
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png" title="Iron pentacarbonyl."><img alt="Iron pentacarbonyl." data-file-height="1112" data-file-width="1011" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png/120px-Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png/180px-Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png/240px-Iron-pentacarbonyl-3D-balls.png 2x" width="120"/></a>
</p><p>Many metal-CO complexes are prepared by decarbonylation of organic solvents, not from CO. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Iridium(III)_chloride" title="Iridium(III) chloride">iridium trichloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triphenylphosphine" title="Triphenylphosphine">triphenylphosphine</a> react in boiling <a href="/wiki/2-Methoxyethanol" title="2-Methoxyethanol">2-methoxyethanol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dimethylformamide" title="Dimethylformamide">DMF</a> to afford <a href="/wiki/Vaska%27s_complex" title="Vaska's complex">IrCl(CO)(PPh<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub></a>.
</p><p>Metal carbonyls in coordination chemistry are usually studied using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy_of_metal_carbonyls" title="Infrared spectroscopy of metal carbonyls">infrared spectroscopy</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_and_main_group_chemistry">Organic and main group chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organic and main group chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the presence of strong acids and water, carbon monoxide reacts with <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acids" title="Carboxylic acids">carboxylic acids</a> in a process known as the Koch–Haaf reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koch_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-koch-83">[83]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gattermann%E2%80%93Koch_reaction" title="Gattermann–Koch reaction">Gattermann–Koch reaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">arenes</a> are converted to <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehyde</a> derivatives in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">AlCl<sub>3</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">HCl</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coleman_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-coleman-84">[84]</a></sup> Organolithium compounds (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butyl_lithium" title="Butyl lithium">butyl lithium</a>) react with carbon monoxide, but these reactions have little scientific use.
</p><p>Although CO reacts with <a href="/wiki/Carbocation" title="Carbocation">carbocations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbanion" title="Carbanion">carbanions</a>, it is relatively nonreactive toward organic compounds without the intervention of metal catalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>With main group reagents, CO undergoes several noteworthy reactions. <a href="/wiki/Halogenation" title="Halogenation">Chlorination</a> of CO is the industrial route to the important compound <a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">phosgene</a>. With <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">borane</a> CO forms the adduct <a href="/wiki/Borane_carbonyl" title="Borane carbonyl">H<sub>3</sub>BCO</a>, which is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acylium" title="Acylium">acetylium</a> cation [H<sub>3</sub>CCO]<sup>+</sup>. CO reacts with <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> to give products resulting from C-C coupling such as <a href="/wiki/Acetylenediol" title="Acetylenediol">sodium acetylenediolate</a> 2<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>·<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. It reacts with molten <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> to give a mixture of an organometallic compound, <a href="/wiki/Acetylenediol" title="Acetylenediol">potassium acetylenediolate</a> 2<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>·<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Benzenehexol" title="Benzenehexol">potassium benzenehexolate</a> 6<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbuechIV_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-wbuechIV-88">[88]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rhodizonic_acid" title="Rhodizonic acid">potassium rhodizonate</a> 2<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>·<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The compounds <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexanehexone" title="Cyclohexanehexone">cyclohexanehexone</a> or triquinoyl (C<sub>6</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentanepentone" title="Cyclopentanepentone">cyclopentanepentone</a> or leuconic acid (C<sub>5</sub>O<sub>5</sub>), which so far have been obtained only in trace amounts, can be regarded as polymers of carbon monoxide.
</p><p>At pressures of over 5 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">gigapascals</a>, carbon monoxide converts into a <a href="/wiki/Polycarbonyl" title="Polycarbonyl">solid polymer of carbon and oxygen</a>.  This is metastable at atmospheric pressure but is a powerful explosive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_industry">Chemical industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Chemical industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is an <a href="/wiki/Industrial_gas" title="Industrial gas">industrial gas</a> that has many applications in bulk chemicals manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Large quantities of aldehydes are produced by the <a href="/wiki/Hydroformylation" title="Hydroformylation">hydroformylation</a> reaction of <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a>, carbon monoxide, and H<sub>2</sub>. Hydroformylation is coupled to the <a href="/wiki/Shell_higher_olefin_process" title="Shell higher olefin process">Shell higher olefin process</a> to give precursors to <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">Phosgene</a>, useful for preparing isocyanates, polycarbonates, and polyurethanes, is produced by passing purified carbon monoxide and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> gas through a bed of porous <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>, which serves as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>.  World production of this compound was estimated to be 2.74 million tonnes in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + Cl<sub>2</sub> → COCl<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">Methanol</a> is produced by the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> of carbon monoxide. In a related reaction, the hydrogenation of carbon monoxide is coupled to C-C bond formation, as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a> where carbon monoxide is hydrogenated to liquid hydrocarbon fuels. This technology allows <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> or biomass to be converted to diesel.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Monsanto_process" title="Monsanto process">Monsanto process</a>, carbon monoxide and <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> react in the presence of a homogeneous <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">rhodium</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroiodic_acid" title="Hydroiodic acid">hydroiodic acid</a> to give <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>. This process is responsible for most of the industrial production of <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>.
</p><p>An industrial scale use for pure carbon monoxide is purifying <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mond_process" title="Mond process">Mond process</a>.
</p><p>Carbon monoxide can also be used in the <a href="/wiki/Water-gas_shift_reaction" title="Water-gas shift reaction">water-gas shift reaction</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meat_coloring">Meat coloring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Meat coloring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Modified_atmosphere" title="Modified atmosphere">modified atmosphere</a> packaging systems in the US, mainly with fresh meat products such as beef, pork, and fish to keep them looking fresh. The carbon monoxide combines with <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> to form carboxymyoglobin, a bright-cherry-red pigment. Carboxymyoglobin is more stable than the oxygenated form of myoglobin, oxymyoglobin, which can become oxidized to the brown pigment <a href="/wiki/Metmyoglobin" title="Metmyoglobin">metmyoglobin</a>. This stable red color can persist much longer than in normally packaged meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meatsci1999_SORHEIM_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meatsci1999_SORHEIM-94">[94]</a></sup> Typical levels of carbon monoxide used in the facilities that use this process are between 0.4% to 0.5%.
</p><p>The technology was first given "<a href="/wiki/Generally_recognized_as_safe" title="Generally recognized as safe">generally recognized as safe</a>" (GRAS) status by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="U.S. Food and Drug Administration">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) in 2002 for use as a secondary packaging system, and does not require labeling. In 2004, the FDA approved CO as primary packaging method, declaring that CO does not mask spoilage odor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meatsci2005_eilert_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meatsci2005_eilert-95">[95]</a></sup> Despite this ruling, the process remains controversial for fears that it masks spoilage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In 2007, a bill<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> was introduced to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> to label modified atmosphere carbon monoxide packaging as a color additive, but the bill died in subcommittee. The process is banned in many other countries, including Japan, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide-releasing_molecules" title="Carbon monoxide-releasing molecules">Carbon monoxide-releasing molecules</a></div>
<p>In biology, carbon monoxide is naturally produced by the action of <a href="/wiki/Heme_oxygenase" title="Heme oxygenase">heme oxygenase</a> 1 and 2 on the <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> breakdown. This process produces a certain amount of carboxyhemoglobin in normal persons, even if they do not breathe any carbon monoxide.
</p><p>Following the first report that carbon monoxide is a normal neurotransmitter in 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verma_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-verma-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-19">[19]</a></sup> as well as one of three gases that naturally modulate inflammatory responses in the body (the other two being <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>), carbon monoxide has received a great deal of clinical attention as a biological regulator. In many tissues, all three gases are known to act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-inflammatories" title="Anti-inflammatories">anti-inflammatories</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasodilators" title="Vasodilators">vasodilators</a>, and encouragers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neovascular" title="Neovascular">neovascular</a> growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2009_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li2009-20">[20]</a></sup> However, the issues are complex, as neovascular growth is not always beneficial, since it plays a role in tumor growth, and also the damage from <i>wet</i> <a href="/wiki/Macular_degeneration" title="Macular degeneration">macular degeneration</a>, a disease for which smoking (a major source of carbon monoxide in the blood, several times more than natural production) increases the risk from 4 to 6 times.
</p><p>There is a theory that, in some nerve cell <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-term_memories" title="Long-term memories">long-term memories</a> are being laid down, the receiving cell makes carbon monoxide, which back-transmits to the transmitting cell, telling it to transmit more readily in future. Some such nerve cells have been shown to contain <a href="/wiki/Guanylate_cyclase" title="Guanylate cyclase">guanylate cyclase</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that is activated by carbon monoxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies involving carbon monoxide have been conducted in many laboratories throughout the world for its anti-inflammatory and cytoprotective properties. These properties have potential to be used to prevent the development of a series of pathological conditions including ischemia reperfusion injury, transplant rejection, atherosclerosis, severe sepsis, severe malaria, or autoimmunity. Clinical tests involving humans have been performed, however the results have not yet been released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clinical-trial_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-clinical-trial-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Metallurgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide is a strong reductive agent, and whilst not known, it has been used in <a href="/wiki/Pyrometallurgy" title="Pyrometallurgy">pyrometallurgy</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a> since ancient times. Carbon monoxide strips oxygen off metal oxides, reducing them to pure metal in high temperatures, forming <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in the process. Carbon monoxide is not usually supplied as is, in gaseous phase, in the reactor, but rather it is formed in high temperature in presence of oxygen-carrying ore, carboniferous agent such as coke and high temperature. The <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> process is a typical example of a process of reduction of metal from ore with carbon monoxide.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lasers">Lasers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Lasers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide has also been used as a <a href="/wiki/Active_laser_medium" title="Active laser medium">lasing medium</a> in high-powered infrared <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niche_uses">Niche uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_monoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Niche uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide has been proposed for use as a fuel on Mars. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide/oxygen_engine" title="Carbon monoxide/oxygen engine">Carbon monoxide/oxygen engines</a> have been suggested for early surface transportation use as both carbon monoxide and oxygen can be straightforwardly produced from the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirconia" title="Zirconia">zirconia</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>, without using any <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">Martian water resources</a> to obtain hydrogen, which would be needed to make methane or any hydrogen-based fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landis2001_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-landis2001-103">[103]</a></sup> Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace_gas" title="Blast furnace gas">blast furnace gas</a> collected at the top of blast furnace, still contains some 10% to 30% of carbon monoxide, and is used as fuel on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cowper_stove" title="Cowper stove">Cowper stoves</a> and on Siemens-Martin furnaces on <a href="/wiki/Open_hearth_furnace" title="Open hearth furnace">open hearth steelmaking</a>.
</p>
